      "title": "Fat Bear Jr! Everything You Need To Know About These Chubby Cubbies!",
      "description": "Mike Fitz & Naomi Boak introduce us to the bears of the first ever Fat Bear Jr. competition! \nLike adult bears, cubs experience many challenges. To prepare for the months-long fast, cubs gain body mass at proportionately greater rates than even the largest adult bears. Only newborn cubs suckle mother’s milk in the den. All others must rely on their fat reserves to survive winter hibernation. A spring (first-year) cub weighs about one pound (0.45 kg) at birth yet may begin hibernation weighing more than 70 pounds (32 kg). By the end of their second summer, yearling cubs in Katmai commonly weigh more than 200 pounds (91 kg).\nBear families in Katmai National Park typically separate at the beginning of a cub’s third or fourth summer. Life with mom is a short apprenticeship, and cubs must absorb mom’s lessons if they are to survive as independent bears.\n\n\nLove Animals and Nature?       "title": "Brown Bears & Wolves hunt Salmon at NIGHT at Brooks Falls!  Watch on infrared cam.  Katmai, Alaska!",
      "description": "The falls platform is closed between 10 p.m. and 7 a.m. from June 15 to August 15 so that bears and other animals have the opportunity to fish for salmon without people nearby.\nInfrared light (which bears and other mammals cannot see) allows our cameras to observe Brooks Falls at night.\nSalmon often continue their migration and spawning efforts under the cover of darkness.\nIn the plunge pools below Brooks Falls, bears fish for salmon by feel. But on the lip of the falls, bears continue to rely on their eyesight throughout the darkest hours to catch salmon.\nThe eyes of bears and wolves are better adapted to see at night compared to humans.\nLike bears, wolves throughout Katmai National Park fish for salmon.\nNighttime can offer wolves the chance to fish with fewer bears nearby and no people to disturb them.\n\nLove Animals and Nature? If we are to return bison to more of the landscape, then effort will not be easy. Bison are large animals with the strength and disposition to injure people and damage property. They can migrate over vast distances, and North America is no longer an open range. They can spread disease to domestic animals and suffer from illnesses our animals give to them. Despite these challenges, making space for bison in parks, preserves, and private lands can not only help to restore an environmental loss but can also play a part in rectifying past injustices suffered by indigenous peoples.
